So let me make this perfectly clear; even though it has it's roots in sci-fi 'Ender's Game' is far more than a novel based in that particular genre.  Andrew 'Ender' Wiggins is a child genius who is recruited, at a very young age, by the military to be trained in the art of war for an ongoing battle with an alien race known as the 'Buggers'. Due to Ender being a genius at such a young age he is shunned by the majority of other kids. Even at the military academy which is full of slightly older children, who are also being trained due to their own high IQs, he is often an object of scorn and jealousy which makes him focus even more on his education. The education often takes the form of various games. These games range from out and out military combat exercises to computer simulations and games that vigorously test his mental skills and reasoning. It's in this psychological aspect that the story excels as we're dragged into Ender's world, Ender's mind, and get to know everything that he is thinking and the logic behind his reasoning. As Ender is very much a sympathetic figure, and constantly bullied, we feel his pain and revel in his triumphs when he outsmarts those who torment him.  Even though Andrew Wiggins is taken away from his family at a young age we never lose touch with his siblings as his older, psychotic brother, and his polar opposite sister who are both geniuses in their own right, form the basis of a back story which intertwines with Ender's story at various points throughout the book. Neither his brother or sister made it to the military academy due to psychological faults. His brother, Peter, is borderline psychotic and prone to constant violent acts. His sister, Valentine, is very passive and both of their personality traits play a major part in the novel.  It's only towards the end that the novel truly becomes sci-fi with space battles and colonizing of new worlds but even if you are not a science fiction fan you will have been hooked long before then and will be turning the pages with such gusto they will leave friction marks on your fingers.  This truly is a great work of literature that deserved the Hugo and Nebula awards which were bestowed upon it. As for Ender you would be hard pressed to find a more wonderfully orchestrated character in any genre of literature; Card, through his words, waves the baton and we eagerly listen attentive and on the edge of our seats to Ender's symphony that the author expertly conducts.

1400052181|2.0|I am surprised by the positive reviews that I see posted.  I disliked this book.  Here's why:    1.  Embedded deeply into the fabric of this book is the author's own ambition.  She is a tenacious reporter.  She wanted a story.  This book is actually about the author's pursuit of her story, her book.  The book is sold to us as a mediation on ethics, genetics and race.  Those subjects are secondary -- and not presented in a particularly interesting way.  For good reason.  As discussed below, there's just not that much good material about which to write.  The issues are straight-forward and not current.  So, instead, we have a book about the author's attempt to gain the confidence of a family so she could obtain strands of information that might be stitched together to form a narrative.  The reader gains the distinct impression that the author gained the confidence of the Lacks family to exploit the Lacks family -- again, so the author would have her book.    2.  The Lacks family (to which the author devotes more than half the book) is not interesting.  Yes, it is poor.  It lives in poverty.  Its members have not had opportunity, try to scratch by and sometimes end up in jail.  Unfortunately, millions of American families share these characteristics.  We know that.  We frequently read about underprivileged families and in many different contexts.  But the Lacks family offers no deep insights.  Its observations about science, justice and money are re-stated over and over again, and they are often steeped in ignorance.    3.  The Lacks family (the protagonists of this story) are not particularly likable.  Its focus is, primarily, money.  Page after page, we read about its desire for compensation.      4.  There's very little in this book that quantities, or even meaningfully explains, how Henrietta Lacks' cells have helped us.  We know they have been used for research.  We know they were sent up into space.  But descriptions of the cells' scientific and medical uses arevague and general.    5.  The central ethical issue presented in this book -- the practice of using peoples' cells for research without their consent -- just isn't that interesting.  The reason is simple:  the cell of an individual isn't the individual himself or herself.  They're cells.  Nothing more.  But the book, while sometimes drawing this distinction, more often conflates the obvious difference between the individual and his or her extracted cells.      6.  The book doesn't have a strong narrative thrust.  There is no suspense, no climax, no denouement.  The book jumps from decade to decade for no obvious reason.  The ending of the book centers around the fate of someone whose motives were mixed and who didn't say a single interesting thing.   I finished the book, but I really had to work at it.  I kept on hoping the ending of the book would bring some satisfaction, but it just fizzled.    7.  There are no clear heroes in the book.  There are no villains.  This reflects well on the author because she obviously didn't bend the truth to make interesting characters. But it makes for a pretty boring book.    8.   The only mystery in the book is left unresolved.  It pertains to money.  The Lacks family wants money.  The author established a foundation for the family.  But there's no indication how the foundation benefits the family.  Rather, the book vaguely states that \\"some of the proceeds of this book are being donated by the author to the Foundation.\\"  So, we are left to wonder.  How much did the Lacks family enrich the author?  How much did she benefit from the Lacks' story -- in much the same way that science and medicine benefited from Henrietta Lacks' cells?  How much money has the Lacks family made as a result of the publication of this best-selling book?  A lot or a little?    9.  The book is poorly edited.  It is redundant is so many ways.  The author repeats the family's conspiracy theories and talks about its resentments dozens of times.  This 300-page book could have been 100 pages shorter -- easily.    10.  The book is condescending to the reader.  It assumes that we will buy, and revel in, a book that purports to be about science, morality and race, but that we will not critically scrutinize whether the book actually delivers.  The fact that this book has been so well reviewed and has appeared on so many best-seller lists suggests that this cynicism was warranted.

B000002G7G|5.0|In 1966 something new and unheard of came out to the masses:  THE VELVET UNDERGROUND & NICO.  Every song on this super-brilliant LP is pure cream.  WHITE LIGHT/WHITE HEAT was ok too, but there was so much experimenting going on that Lou's brilliant songwriting was lost in the mix.  But their 3rd, self-titled LP shows Lou's writing only rising and rising, pure musical poetry - Doug Yule, who replaced John Cale, sings vocals on the opening track, \\"Candy Says\\".  \\"Jesus\\" is a strange song - what the hell does it mean?  Do you take it at face value and think \\"ohmygod\\", Lou's turning into a monk?  Or is it, instead, metaphorical, which would make it an apt tune for the time -after the \\"flower power\\" years of '65-'67, assassinations, bigotry, riots and the Vietnam War all coalesced to turn a once high, pie-in-the-sky ideal and twisted it into anthems of anger, isolation, anti-war protests and more self-centeredness, less universal \\"love & peace\\" - this was the epitome of disillusionment.<br />  Also, LSD was the drug that opened up consciousnesses, woke people up to what is real and helped the brain to see that crass commercialism is just as bad as the military-industrial complex, since, even back then there was synergy among behemoth corps.-from bongos to bombs.  From \\"nice\\" to Napalm, etc (for a great example of this anger/frustration, listen to \\"21st Century Schizoid Man\\" on King Crimson's debut, IN THE COURT OF THE CRIMSON KING (a few years ago Bad Religion came out w/a song \\"21st Century Digital Boy\\" -didn't really think much about it until I happened to hear it close-up and clearly - it's not a \\"cover\\" of the former, but, rather an \\"homage?\\" to King Crimson's tune - there's a line in the Bad Religion tune where Greg Gaffin sings a line from KC's tune:  \\"Innocents raped with Napalm fire...\\", that's when I knew what they were up to.<br />  But, I digress.  THE VELVET UNDERGROUND (Verve Records) was the last great studio LP the Velvets made as LOADED was full of pop-songs, silly goofs, etc.  It's like Lou Reed was parodying some of the banal pop crap that was eking its way out.  But Lou Reed, unlike most songwriters, can craft a beautiful song (\\"Pale Blue Eyes\\", \\"Candy Says\\", \\"Jesus\\", \\"I'll be Your Mirror\\", etc) and he can also lay down the most perfect \\"pop\\" songs, e.g., \\"Sweet Jane\\", \\"Rock & Roll\\", \\"Femme Fatale\\" and \\"After Hours\\", to name a few.<br />  After they broke up & went their separate ways, Lou Reed, it seems, had no intention of going away.  After a little rest he met David \\"genius\\" Bowie and the two of them recorded Lou's solo debut, TRANSFORMER, with Bowie getting credit as \\"Producer\\".  Besides just hanging about in the studio \\"supervising\\" (???) Bowie also shows up on the LP TRANSFORMER:  being an accomplished sax player, Bowie is the one who plays the saxophone solo at the end of the now ubiquitously famous anthem of transvestites, hustling and drug addiction, \\"Walk On the Wild Side\\".  Bowie can also be heard singing background vocals on \\"Satellite of Love\\" - towards the end of the tune, when the refrain is being sung over & over until fade-out, you hear \\"Satellite...OH, OH, OH, OH\\".  The \\"Oh-oh-oh-oh's\\", if you remember are a little high pitched (to high for Lou)but it's no woman and no unknown -it's Bowie!  For those who don't know what I mean or who want to hear it for themselves, well, dig out your copy of TRANSFORMER and listen to \\"Satellite of Love\\" at a high volume - it may take a couple listens to recognize it, but that IS Bowie, crooning along with Lou at the end.<br />  After TRANSFORMER, which was recorded in London, Lou conceived & wrote BERLIN, a \\"concept\\" album, which, initially confused & confounded critics & fans alike.  No one knew what to make of it; it lacked the crafty pop stuff that Reed had been famous for, among other feats.  No, BERLIN was a dreary, depressing dirge-like album that revolved around screwed-up relationships and co-dependency (redundant?)  So, at 1st BERLIN didn't do very well, commercially and even many long-time fans were bewildered, threw their collective hands in the air, shrugged their shoulders and moved on to what would become 2 1970s hallmarks:  Disco and Punk, the latter being in debt to Lou Reed, Iggy Pop, Bowie, MC5 and Blue Cheer.  Disco was a result of bad decision making at record labels such as Casablanca, where the producers & engineers were doing mountains of cocaine, heroin, speed and the good old classics, Nembutal, Seconal (reds) and Tuinol (found in Elvis's bathroom medicine chest after he died).  Don't forget Quaaludes and Sopors, heavy-duty sedative-hypnotics.<br />  But times change and so do tastes & understanding:  BERLIN is now considered, by most Lou fans, as one of his best works.  \\"Oh Jim\\" -into \\"Sad Song\\" are my faves on BERLIN.  But the best versions of those two cuts can be found on LOU REED LIVE (not ROCK 'N' ROLL ANIMAL) - the live \\"Oh Jim\\" is about 10 minutes long, as opposed to the LP version, which clocks in at around five 1/2 minutes.<br />  After that, heroin addiction and other demons in his life started to impact his music.  Of course, you could blame it on the times, ROCK 'N' ROLL HEART, GROWING UP IN PUBLIC, etc, were mediocre at best.  It just sounded like Lou was tired of the self-one-ups-manship of putting out better, fresher stuff every couple years, so, probably to fulfill contractual obligations, he just went through the motions and cranked out these quite introspective, but in the end, just not the same.  One exception to this was the 1978 release, STREET HASSLE, which is one of the most underrated LPs of all time; the vinyl LP's been out of print for a long time, but with CDs now, it's been re-released for you big fans.  The title cut even features a short, spoken-word lyric sung by Bruce Springsteen!  It's not sung, but you can hear \\"The Boss\\" mumbling the words over a cool bass jam near the end of the song.<br />Unfortunately, it wouldn't be until 1989 that Lou would put out one of his best efforts yet:  NEW YORK.  This was a concept album too - in fact, in the liner notes, Lou writes \\"this is meant to be played & listened to all the way through-at one sitting...\\" and indeed, it's true and the album is magic.<br />  But back to the VU's third LP - a timeless, classic that still sounds great today.  If you haven't heard it, I'd strongly suggest you go out & find it, it'll be worth your time!<br />  Happy trails,<br />  KMANTHIEcommercially and even many long-time fans were bewildered, threw their collective hands in the air, shrugged their shoulders and moved on to what would become 2 1970s hallmarks:  Disco and Punk, the latter being in debt to Lou Reed, Iggy Pop, Bowie, MC5 and Blue Cheer.  Disco was a result of bad decision making at record labels such as Casablanca, where the producers & engineers were doing mountains of cocaine, heroin, speed and the good old classics, Nembutal, Seconal (reds) and Tuinol (found in Elvis's bathroom medicine chest after he died).  Don't forget Quaaludes and Sopors, heavy-duty sedative-hypnotics.      But times change and so do tastes & understanding:  BERLIN is now considered, by most Lou fans, as one of his best works.  \\"Oh Jim\\" -into \\"Sad Song\\" are my faves on BERLIN.  But the best versions of those two cuts can be found on LOU REED LIVE (not ROCK 'N' ROLL ANIMAL) - the live \\"Oh Jim\\" is about 10 minutes long, as opposed to the LP version, which clocks in at around five 1/2 minutes.        After that, heroin addiction and other demons in his life started to impact his music.  Of course, you could blame it on the times, ROCK 'N' ROLL HEART, GROWING UP IN PUBLIC, etc, were mediocre at best.  It just sounded like Lou was tired of the self-one-ups-manship of putting out better, fresher stuff every couple years, so, probably to fulfill contractual obligations, he just went through the motions and cranked out these quite introspective, but in the end, just not the same.  One exception to this was the 1978 release, STREET HASSLE, which is one of the most underrated LPs of all time; the vinyl LP's been out of print for a long time, but with CDs now, it's been re-released for you big fans.  The title cut even features a short, spoken-word lyric sung by Bruce Springsteen!  It's not sung, but you can hear \\"The Boss\\" mumbling the words over a cool bass jam near the end of the song.  Unfortunately, it wouldn't be until 1989 that Lou would put out one of his best efforts yet:  NEW YORK.  This was a concept album too - in fact, in the liner notes, Lou writes \\"this is meant to be played & listened to all the way through-at one sitting...\\" and indeed, it's true and the album is magic.       But back to the VU's third LP - a timeless, classic that still sounds great today.  If you haven't heard it, I'd strongly suggest you go out & find it, it'll be worth your time!      Happy trails,                  KMANTHIE

0618680004|5.0|An interesting point has been brought up by some theologians and secular philosophers about this book: It doesn't use the best arguments against God's existence. Not that the arguments in here aren't good, but they aren't always the best. I am not a philosopher (except in an amateur sense), so I imagine they could give a pretty good run down of why these explanations aren't the best.     The \\"Where did God come from?/Who designed the designer?\\" argument:    If one is attempting to explain the origins of the universe, then claiming it comes from some un-caused cause is rather arbitrary, one could just as easily claim the universe itself was un-caused and therefore save a step (Occam's Razor) and arrive at the same conclusion (and no, the Kalam Cosmological Argument doesn't succeed is showing the universe had a cause).   William Lane Craig has criticized this argument by saying (paraphrase) \\"If this is your approach to explanation, then you will get an infinite regress, and never come to any answer, thereby destroying science\\" which isn't entirely true. Dawkins points out on pg 188, \\"The whole problem we started out with was the problem of explaining statistical improbability.\\" By positing natural selection as a \\"consciousness raiser,\\" he has shown that there are processes that can account for such improbability. A process doesn't need to be explained separately from its definition. One can ask \\"Where did the designer come from?\\" and have to resort to some other, external explanation or some kind of metaphysical jargon about being a \\"necessary being.\\" But to ask \\"Where did the process come from?\\" one need only point out that since it's a process, it is simply the result of various things interacting. A process isn't a \\"thing\\" that needs to be explained separately from what it does. Dawkins uses evolution as an example of the explanatory power of such processes in biology, and points out that there are probably similar explanations in other scientific fields, even if they haven't been discovered yet. This is why positing an intelligent designer is problematic.  Additionally, an infinite regress is not a problem in science. Since science is about discovering mysteries and explaining things, an infinite regress would allow science to go on forever. What WOULD destroy science is to say \\"THIS is the ONLY explanation, end of discussion.\\"      Additionally, his case against the ontological argument has been criticized by some, who have (rightly) pointed out that it has puzzled philosophers for centuries, and that it's rather patronizing of Dawkins to be so cavalier with it. I don't think this is a problem myself, as I also find it to be absurd.   Dawkins points out on pg. 107, \\"My own feeling, to the contrary, would have been an automatic suspicion of any line of reasoning that reached such a significant conclusion without feeding in a single piece of data from the real world. Perhaps that indicates no mare than that I am a scientist rather than a philosopher.\\" None of the criticisms against this book that I have read have addressed this statement, which is a neat summery of why philosophy itself often gets a bad rap: it is often disconnected from the real world; science has done more to aid the human condition in the last 400 or so years than 2000+ years of philosophy has.        Dawkins blows the teleological (fine-tuning) argument clean out of the water, and demolishes the argument from personal experience. Both are popular arguments used for the existence of any deity.    This book has done more for the case of atheism and getting people to realize we don't have to sit by and deal with theocratic encroachment on our lives then almost all the \\"proper\\" atheistic philosophy has done. People aren't likely to go out and read \\"The Impossibility of God\\" as summer reading, much less understand much of it without some formal philosophical training. But \\"The God Delusion,\\" while not perfect, is accessible and enjoyable.   To make an analogy:Napoleon was an incredible general, but even he needed an army to get anything done. The best atheist arguments in philosophy are like Napoleon sulking in his room. Dawkins provides the army, the accessibility, for these arguments to be known in the world.

Maybe its my eyesight - but frankly I could not see much for most of the film.   Either nighttime or in the dark.  Oh well - I listened.  Very disappointed.

B002YV7P10|5.0|I don't know why this title was never released in a UK or American edition. I'm afraid I can't tell you whether this edition is in English or Spanish or whether it is good quality or not. I have the Dutch edition (which you can find [[ASIN:B0018UZCVS here]]. Both are Region 2 so they won't play in a regular DVD player.<br /><br />This film is a BBC Docudrama produced in 2006 and starring Alexander Siddig (Dr. Bashir from Star Trek: DS9) as Hannibal, and no one else I know except for Ben Cross (Sarek, also from Star Trek). I have never understood the use of docudramas. If you want to make a documentary, make a documentary; if you want to make a period piece, make a period piece. Combining the two does not work. It is not as accurate as a documentary or as exciting as a drama. But the BBC was quite fond of churning them out in the middle of the decade. Within a few years they threw out at least than three of them set at least partially in ancient times: [[ASIN:B0022SFCO2 Ancient Rome: The Rise and Fall of an Empire]], Heroes & Villains (Released in the US as [[ASIN:B001O2UTT0 Warriors]]. Was the original name too confusing?), and this one. Of them all I think that this one is the most coherent. Instead of a bunch of shorter episodes dealing with different people, it spends the whole time looking at the Carthaginian general Hannibal. No, not that Hannibal. This one doesn't eat people (that we know of). Hannibal was the man who almost took down Rome itself. He crossed the Alps and brought the fight to them culminating in one of the most decisive battles the world has ever seen. Yet the Romans survived and lived to fight another day.<br /><br />So, how well does it do? Historically it gets a few things wrong and compresses some events to make them fit into an hour and a half program but is very accurate on the whole. Entertainment-wise it is also very good. Some of the dialogue is clunky and phony but it doesn't distract from the story too much. The effects seem about normal for a docudrama at first. In most scenes you can tell that there are only a few dozen men playing soldiers. The battle of Ticinus features about two dozen men on each side wailing at each other with swords. That's about what I expected from the production, and it's dull. Then it reaches Cannae and everything changes. Cannae is Hannibal's big battle, the one that would define his career. He fought a Roman army that outnumbered him and managed to not only defeat it, but obliterate it killing about 50,000 men in the course of a few hours. And this film captures it just right. I really don't know how they did it. It must be CGI because they could never have afforded that many extras, but it doesn't look like any CGI work I've ever seen. It details exactly what tactics were used to win the battle and illustrates them perfectly. I don't think I've seen a better (or more accurate) battle on-screen before, with the possible exception of Oliver Stone's [[ASIN:B000MGB6NM Alexander]]. The battle of Zama is similarly well done with some believable elephant charges. Both of them are too short unfortunately, but that's a television budget for you. They could not have done better with what they had. A superb job.<br /><br />Apart from the battles, the story's quite good since it follows the facts pretty well and the facts are pretty cool. The first part (45 minutes) covers Hannibal crossing the Alps and kinda rushes through the battles of Trebia and Trasimene (Trasimene in particular is just a voiceover with stills of dead Romans). The second half contains both of the main battles and is infinitely the better for it. Hannibal's character is portrayed somewhat inconsistently. While he starts off as a living embodiment of vengeance after Cannae he starts talking about how too much blood has been spilled and becomes a bit of a whiner. While this isn't an utterly invalid view I don't think that they handled it too well. The turnabout is too sudden and it is unconvincing to hear him talk about not taking Rome because they're not barbarians. It's not really an ancient attitude and it seems to be thrown in there to make him more sympathetic.<br /><br />The casting is a bit of a problem. Siddig is great as Hannibal who comes across as both fierce and brilliant, but most of the supporting cast can't keep up. I guess there aren't many British Arabic actors to choose from which is why Hannibal's brothers look nothing alike. Then again, the Roman cast isn't much better. Cross does fine as Fabius, but the guy playing Scipio is far too old for the part. He's at least in his mid-30s and it seems strange to hear people talking about how young he is to have achieved what he did. It makes sense age-wise by Zama, but that only gives him ten minutes or so at the proper age. Hannibal should be younger too of course, but his age wasn't so significant as Scipio's since Rome had to bend all their laws to give Scipio the command. And it's also strange that a man who's most defining characteristic in his sculptures is his baldness is played by a man with far too much hair for a Roman. Most odd. But I'm nitpicking now. On the whole the film is good, the story is good, the lead performances are good, and the battles are great. The narration is only occasionally present and while it's annoying it shouldn't get in the way of enjoying the film. So check it out if you get the chance, and hopefully they'll release it in Region 1 someday.Rome because they're not barbarians. 0312330871|3.0|Agatha Christie's 1939 story idea captures the imagination. Ten strangers who each, in his or her own way, have gotten away with murder gather by invitation at an isolated mansion. Then their unknown host systematically and mockingly murders them one by one. The idea was adapted into a film in 1945, 1965, 1974, and 1989, as well as a TV adaption in 1959 and a Russian production (often credited as the truest to the book, though hard to get a good copy of that is not too dark and muffled).  Variants of the plot are perennials in movies and TV, appearing, for example, in Harry O, Quincy, and Remington Steele. As someone who has read all of Christie's work and admires it as running the gamut from diverting to entertaining to inspiring, I had high expectations. Unfortunately, neither Christie nor the filmmakers succeeded in bringing this brilliant idea to life in a truly fulfilling story.  Occasionally, a mystery plot can be so ingenious and powerful that it can be a satisfying story in itself. Most often, the best that is achieved by plot alone is an amusing but arid puzzle mystery. For the depth and richness to engage, a novel usually needs to develop to some meaningful degree the characters, their backgrounds, their interaction, their dialogue, the setting, the tone, and any larger themes.<br /><br />The premise of Christie's book is fascinating. As to plot, the book is clever in conception and careful in execution. Compared to the films, the book's assortment of past crimes and depictions of the characters' attitudes toward them are more varied, subtle, and interesting. The order of the crimes has some meaning.  The book does the best job of presenting the characters in ways in which the reader could actually see them as the murderous host. It is the least sentimental, treating all of them vaguely and suspiciously. This is not necessarily enough to make them convincing killers, but at least it maintains more of a sense of menace, suspense, and purpose than the film versions. The book does the best job of explaining why and how the host carried out the scheme.<br /><br />Yet, even the plot has definite shortcomings. Once the imaginative premise is established, the story becomes thin and formulaic. There is little plot or character development. The characters in the book engage in less deductive reasoning and survival techniques than in the 1945 film. The only real plot twist creates a major logical problem, which the book tries to overcome by implausibly suggesting that the ploy would either trick or \\"rattle\\" the murderer. The guests' murders are designed to follow the nursery rhyme, but, aside from some cosmetic frills, the poisoning, stabbing, shooting, and head-bashing that go on in the book show, in themselves, no special cunning, skill, strategic advantage, or plausibility. The killer strikes crudely without detection too effortlessly.<br /><br />The storytelling seems flat, frigid, and, at times, slow-paced. There is no lead character to care about. Characters are described largely by catch-phrases (the judge's \\"tortoise-like\\" appearance; Lombard's \\"wolfish smile\\") or hardly at all (Marston, the Rogerses). Their backgrounds and motives are sketched in summary fashion. The past crimes vary widely in originality, depth, and genuineness. Some -- especially Claythorne's and the general's -- are more subtle, interesting, and powerful than others. Some, like Blore's, Dr. Armstrong's, and Lombard's, are utterly trite, unexplored, and ineffective.<br /><br />However, the book's and films' worst failing is that they have nothing serious to say about the powerful themes that are at the very heart of the story. The story is inherently an observation of human nature in a desperate situation. How do the characters behave? How do they try to reason? How do they try to survive? Also by its very nature -- as the book's last pages acknowledge -- this story is a morality play. How is each of the characters a \\"criminal\\"? How is each \\"beyondthe law\\"? Does each get \\"justice\\"? Is justice the point, or simply a \\"lust\\" to torture and kill? Is the story about breaking the law or enforcing it, about mistakes or abuses in pursuing justice? None of this is meaningfully addressed.<br /><br />The films are worse in some respects and better in some respects than the book. Each is worth seeing as an attempt to bring to life a compelling but at the same time confining plot concept. Each, like the book, fails to fully realize that idea as a story.<br /><br />Briefly, the 1945 film develops the plot better in some ways than the book and is more entertaining. The cast is outstanding, except Mischa Auer. But the film presents the general and his past crime ineffectively and changes others. Its attempts to make the characters entertaining come at the expense of their plausibility as villains and of the story's seriousness. The climactic scene revealing the host's identity, means, and motives is short, sedate, and unsatisfying.<br /><br />The 1965 film is enjoyable and energetic. But it is not as tightly and richly told, nor as well-acted, as the 1945 version, despite good casting. As in 1945, attempts to make characters comical or appealing sap the suspense. The final scene has more explanation than in 1945, but remains thin and undramatic.<br /><br />The 1974 film takes a different tone than its predecessors. It captures more of a sense of fear, dread, and suspense. Yet, overall, the movie is less substantial and entertaining than the prior versions. The storytelling is so spare and unartful that it is sterile and uninvolving. Other than Stephane Audran, the outstanding cast is unable to breathe life into the characters. The final scene is less effective than before.<br /><br />Everything about the 1989 version seems low-budget, from the cast to the script to the set to the production values. The casting of the doctor is even worse than Frank Stallone as Lombard. And not until Donald Pleasance's final moments on screen does he play his character with any coherence. But Herbert Lom is excellent as the general. And it is left to this film to finally present a dramatic end scene.\\"? Is justice the point, or simply a \\"lust\\" to torture and kill? Is the story about breaking the law or enforcing it, about mistakes or abuses in pursuing justice? None of this is meaningfully addressed.    The films are worse in some respects and better in some respects than the book. Each is worth seeing as an attempt to bring to life a compelling but at the same time confining plot concept. Each, like the book, fails to fully realize that idea as a story.    Briefly, the 1945 film develops the plot better in some ways than the book and is more entertaining. The cast is outstanding, except Mischa Auer. But the film presents the general and his past crime ineffectively and changes others. Its attempts to make the characters entertaining come at the expense of their plausibility as villains and of the story's seriousness. The climactic scene revealing the host's identity, means, and motives is short, sedate, and unsatisfying.    The 1965 film is enjoyable and energetic. But it is not as tightly and richly told, nor as well-acted, as the 1945 version, despite good casting. As in 1945, attempts to make characters comical or appealing sap the suspense. The final scene has more explanation than in 1945, but remains thin and undramatic.    The 1974 film takes a different tone than its predecessors. It captures more of a sense of fear, dread, and suspense. Yet, overall, the movie is less substantial and entertaining than the prior versions. The storytelling is so spare and unartful that it is sterile and uninvolving. Other than Stephane Audran, the outstanding cast is unable to breathe life into the characters. The final scene is less effective than before.    Everything about the 1989 version seems low-budget, from the cast to the script to the set to the production values. The casting of the doctor is even worse than Frank Stallone as Lombard. And not until Donald Pleasance's final moments on screen does he play his character with any coherence. But Herbert Lom is excellent as the general. And it is left to this film to finally present a dramatic end scene.

0316017922|3.0|Read the Epilogue first.  This will help you \\"get it\\".  Because of his Jamaican ancestry, Gladwell is familiar with the importance of skin shade and the era in which one is born in determining achievement; lighter skinned slaves worked indoors and lived a longer happier life than dark skinned slaves--and the degree of lightness or darkness of skin mattered (i.e. white and light, olive, light brown, dark brown and black).    Gladwell theorizes that parentage and benefaction control individual success; when and where one grows up, cultural legacies and special opportunities are the real-world factors that determine extraordinary achievement; inherent talent plays less of a role than preparation.  Researchers can, says Gladwell, predict which countries are best at math, without testing students, by determining which nations place the highest emphasis on effort and hard work and do not give schoolchildren long summer vacations.  Gladwell's   citation of research is sparse at times and his interpretation of the results of the research is debatable.   According to Gladwell, researchers have found that excellence at performing a complex task requires a minimal level of 10,000 hours of practice. This amount of practice is only possible if a person is afforded an extraordinary opportunity such as was enjoyed by Bill Joy, Bill Gates, and The Beatles.  The opportunities, and the strength and presence of mind to use them-- not extraordinary talent or intelligence--determine who will be an Outlier and achieve phenomenal success; not even the most gifted can escape the limitations of their generation or place in history.    Gladwell's definition of meaningful work perplexed me until I took into account the Jamaican slaves from whom he descended. He describes work in the garment industry and in rice paddies as meaningful because of the autonomy, complexity and connection between effort and reward; compared to slavery these are indeed meaningful occupations.    What does this allmean to Gladwell?  In order to build a better world, humankind should strive to replace the patchwork of opportunities with opportunities for all. This concept, though difficult to fully implement, is not new;  Gladwell succeeds in renewing my commitment to this ideal.

Price Tag: The most well-known track on the album, this definitely gets a boost from some great Dr. Luke production. Overall, it has a nice island vibe with B.o.B. dropping a solid rap. A very pleasant song. B    Nobody's Perfect: This track rises most due to Jessie's invested singing, paired with a solid hook and some fairly well-written lyrics. Definite single potential. B+    Abracadabra: Dr. Luke gives some more solid production, but this has some weak writing that makes it a little weaker than its predecessor. Another good track, but not exceptional by any means. B    Big White Room: This track may have sounded better as a bonus to end the album; right here it stops the album dead. A vocally profound performance with some very nice lyrics, but this one suffers a bit from unnecessary length. B-    Casualty of Love: Another song held up by Jessie's vocals. The writing is okay here, and the production is inoffensive, but not mind-blowing. B    Rainbow: This one offers a bit of a rap break, and has the most catchy chorus of any song on the album. A shiny gem with an uplifting message. B+    Who's Laughing Now: There's a bit of grating spoken beats in the middle, but overall this is another solid song. Instantly catchy, but maybe not in a good way. B    Do It Like a Dude: One of the best songs on the album, this is a track stuffed full of attitude. Jessie does an awesome jobselling it, and it's a shame this one didn't take off more. B+    Momma Knows Best: The weakest track on the album. The production could have been better if it didn't seem so left-field that it distracted from the premise. It doesn't help that it is also lyrically weak. C    L.O.V.E.: This doesn't help to pick the album back up, with lyrics that just drag the album further down. Not a bad song, but it suffers from placement on the track listing. C+    Stand Up: Another uplifting track, this builds a bit of steam for the album. An unoriginal concept with production that turns out just okay, this one fades in a bit. B    I Need This: One of the most vocally strong and emotionally invested songs, this track should have switched places with 'Big White Room'. A very strong performance by Jessie on this one, with some nice lyrics to match. B+    Who You Are: A great track to end the album on. This one leaves the listener thinking the album is better then it actually was, which is always a nice effect. Jessie's vocals are strong, the lyrics are uplifting (a running trend here, it seems), and the production doesn't overpower her. B+    There's no doubt that Jessie J will be successful. 'Price Tag' is likely to place in the top 10 on Billboard, and there are enough adequate songs her to keep her momentum going until her sophomore release. Adequate, though, is really the issue with this album. Only a few songs manage to surpass this status, and it's always by a small margin. The selling point is really Jessie J's vocals; there isn't anything here that a female vocalist, UK or US bred, hasn't done either equally or better.    Recommended Tracks: Price Tag, Nobody's Perfect, Do It Like a Dude, I Need This, Who You Are

B003YD00RS|5.0|The Checklist Manifesto, by Atul Gawande, tells a remarkable story about how a simple tool - a checklist - could change the way many of us work in the future. As our work becomes more and more complex, having a checklist to follow might literally save our lives. It will most certainly save us money. The book is full of remarkable stories, in fact, and they all have that one little thing in common. A checklist.<br />  According to Gawande, in many industries complexity has increased far beyond what even the most extensive training can prepare any one person to cope with on their own. Gawande uses a construction site for a high rise commercial building to show how the building industry has evolved from the \\"Master Builder\\" age, where one person was responsible for the design of a project and then personally oversaw the execution of all aspects of it, to a coordinated group effort requiring the expertise of many specialists. And he points to medicine as an example of a field still trying to make the \\"Master Builder\\" model work.<br />  When a delegate from the World Health Organization (WHO) asked Gawande for his help in devising a way to make surgeries safer in developing nations, he at first didn't see how it could be done. He had, by that time, seen that checklists could help reduce mistakes in some medical procedures, but considered surgery to have too many variables for a checklist to take into account. The checklist would be inconveniently long, or too vague, and would ultimately waste time. Still, he saw more instances where use of checklists in hospitals was lowering the incidence of surgical complications. Fewer complications saved lives, got patients home from the hospital sooner, and saved everyone money. Gawande decided to get his team together  and try again to develop a workable checklist. The results of their efforts I'll leave for you to discover. I'm sure you won't be disappointed.

He then looked at what these mom with the healthiest children did. He found his \\"bright spots\\" and transformed the nutrition of the entire village. That simple switch improved nutrition in the village. Adding a little protein to the diet, helped even more.<br /><br />From Switch,I learned to look for bright spots. 0060557818|2.0|This is one of those books I zip through, only to realize upon completion that the book isn't actually very good. But since some of Gaiman's other work has been very well-received, perhaps this just wasn't the right place for me to start.<br /><br />As other reviewers have described, Neverwhere is about a young man living in modern-day London, who through an act of kindness toward a stranger gets sucked into the world of London Below, where people live dangerous lives in the tunnels and Tube stations below the city and are nearly invisible to the inhabitants of London Above. He joins a girl named Door and her companions on their quest to find out the truth behind Door's family's murders.<br /><br />In all fairness, this book just wasn't my cup of tea. First, because it's essentially an urban-fantasy-thriller; my impression of Gaiman was that he's a more literary writer--and okay, there's a Shakespeare reference or two, but this book is still a thriller, with a fast-paced but unmemorable plot consisting of constant rushing about the tunnels of London Below, and characters who are decent and likeable but not particularly well-developed. Second, I didn't realize that the book was adapted from a TV series, and it reads a bit like a video game: the plot consists mostly of running around and questing after various items, there are lots of tired tropes (the murdered family in the backstory, the mysterious waif with magical powers, the mysterious manipulative villain) and the end is predictable. Silliest was the important plot location that, we're solemnly informed, can only be accessed \\"the easy way\\" once by any individual; the second visit must be via an underground labyrinth. Naturally, this is never explained; do plot coupons, dungeons and boss battles need to be explained? We all understand that the point is to give the player a chance to... but wait a minute, this is a novel, not a role-playing game.<br /><br />The setting, meanwhile, is an interesting concept but not fully developed; like the protagonist, I feel like I was whisked through a maze of tunnels, but have no idea how to get anywhere and am not entirely convinced they still exist when I'm not there. And Gaiman explicitly raises a lot of questions that are never answered (the magic is never explained at all, nor are any limitations placed on it beyond the requirements of the plot; the villains don't seem to be human, though we're never told what they are). The parallels drawn between the citizens of London Below and homeless people (\\"those who fall through the cracks\\") are interesting; I'm just not sure that they actually work. After all, the Below-ers are literally invisible to the Above-ers, and there's some implication that the Below-ers lead more interesting or meaningful lives. On the other hand, part of the point of fantasy is to comment on reality, and the analogies can't always be exact.<br /><br />So I didn't think much of this book, and am not sure if I'll try a different Gaiman book in the future; but I recognize that it may just be me, and if you're looking for a quick fantasy adventure (but not necessarily light; it's gruesome in places) this may be just the book for you.he protagonist, I feel like I was whisked through a maze of tunnels, but have no idea how to get anywhere and am not entirely convinced they still exist when I'm not there. On the other hand, part of the point of fantasy is to comment on reality, and the analogies can't always be exact.    So I didn't think much of this book, and am not sure if I'll try a different Gaiman book in the future; but I recognize that it may just be me, and if you're looking for a quick fantasy adventure (but not necessarily light; it's gruesome in places) this may be just the book for you.

B000000OAN|5.0|For a long time, I avoided Queen.  Aside from avidly listening to Bohemian Rhapsody the day Freddie Mercury died, and occasionaly listening to the first two Greatest Hits (ignore the third - not remotely comparable to the first two), I was put off by the brassy camp, the kitsch self-knowingness, a certain smarminess and not a small dose of synths in their 80s output.  Then something odd happened.    I downloaded a few of their rockier tracks on itunes - 'Dragon Attack', 'Brighton Rock' and so on - and one day realised that I actually rather liked what I heard.    Next, I listened to the album Jazz.  A strange album, Jazz.  At least, that is, when you're used to the rather more serious likes of Led Zep, Neil Young, and a raft of other 60s/70s big names.  It's first track, 'Mustapha' seemed just that little bit too absurd.  The later 'Let me entertain you' was just too clever.  I wondered if there was much else left once the humour evaporated...    So, I tried 'A Night at the Opera'.  Prior to recording it, Queen had been swindled by their previous manager and, with new management and a sense of throwing everything but the kitchen sink in, they recorded a masterpiece.  Like Jazz, this is a strange beast.  Stylistically, it veers all over the place.  Side One, goes from venomous and overblown hard rock to Ivor Novello-ish music hall to a sonic belter to a sweet love song with gorgeous harmonies to an echoey Graham Nash-like folky ditty to a somewhat generic hard rock no - that only really cuts loose to the end - to a frankly hilarious treat ('Seaside Rendezvous').  Side Two begins with a Townshend/Lloyd Webber-like rock opera, with a startling a capella interlude, then segues into very moving balladry, then moves to an homage to 1940s taste (ukeleles and jazz bands) then into Bohemian Rhapsody.  Finally, there's a guitar orchestra take on 'God save the Queen'.    I suppose the easiest comparison would be something like The Beatles' 'White Album'.  With Queen trailing The Beatles as the second most popular band in the UK ever (at least by sales), perhaps it's this ability to incorporate the many moods of life onto record that proves a hit with the British record buying public.      With that thought, I 'got' Queen.  Too unashamedly English (with all that the sense of humour entails), they could not sit contentedly as just another 70s cock-rock/glam rock band (for those of you not from the British Isles, perhaps the worst crime possible in the UK is lacking the British dry-to-mocking sense of humour and appearing just that liitle bit too sincere - hence why U2 and Coldplay have many haters in the UK).  Not to say that without Mercury, that could well have happened (witness the sometimes atrocious contributions of Roger Taylor).  In Mercury, they had the single most charismatic frontman in music history, but he also had perhaps the greatest range and tone of any rock singer (at least, by convention) and was a sensitive and original pianist.    Apparently, Brian May and Roger Taylor used to argue like brothers.  Mercury took a calming role and John Deacon avoided the fray.  I like to imagine the frantic efforts that went into 'Night at the Opera' - it took longer to record than any album prior to it.  Just think about that for a moment.  Consider the landmarks before it - 'Pet Sounds', 'Sgnt Peppers','Dark Side of the Moon'... it's quite a feat.  Not for Queen the turgid months that went into, say, Fleetwood Mac's (later) 'Tusk'. 'Opera' sounds taught and focused - even in the somewhat lengthy 'Prophet's song'.    So, to recap - buy this album.  If you can, buy it on vinyl - it looks and, in my view at least, sounds better on vinyl.  You get the originally desired package that way, the historical artifact.  It's a hoot, it's moving, it's stirring, it takes you to some strange places and brings you back home and slightly more spaced out.  And it made me like Queen, which is saying something.   Oh, and I really like 'Jazz' now, by theway.    Best tracks: I'm in love with my car, You're my best friend, Seaside Rendezvous, Love of my life, and Bohemian Rhapsody.

Microphone Quality, 4 of 5 - Good overall, better than my cell phone.    Noise isolation, 3 of 5 - These are natural (passive) noise-canceling headphones, as far as I can tell. For not using an active signal to block incoming noise, they perform very well. They also won't give you a splitting headache after hours of use, which is a plus to me. Some people will expect the same performance as Bose's $300 headphones, but you won't find that here (and should not be a major selling point either).     Range, 4 of 5 - As I mentioned in the first category, sound is excellent. The high range pops well, the mid range is mellow and full, and bass has some decent kick. I just wanted a little bit extra fullness from the bass, but that is barely distracting.    Style, 5 of 5 - This is very subjective, but I like it. The wireless is great, the pulldown boom mic works well, and the glossy black works with the matte black and the red accents. Leather ear cups are a nice touch too.    Durability, 5 of 5 - They feel heavy enough that they shouldn't break (like a pair of the cheaper Bose headphones did on me).     Fitment, 4 of 5 - Different for each person, but my head is rather average sized (in the military I wore a M helmet; L just for extra protection) and even on the smallest setting, there was space between my head and the head band.      Summary:    Pros: Great sound, wireless, good quality, sexy.  Cons: Bass is a smidge less powerful than I thought it should be, not active noise canceling, no equalizer presets.    Bottom line: Would buy again!

B003VANOFY|5.0|Way back in 2002 when surround sound on computers meant quadraphonic, and Zalman was just getting founded as a company: I created a pair of DIY surround sound gaming headphones by modifying a pair AKG 280 headphones.  Since that day I have only played games using surround sound headphones of one sort or another.    For the past few years I have used a pair of Turtle Beach HPA2 headphones. They have worked well, and still sound great to this day, except they are simply falling apart from use. I was a bit hesitant to give up my XFI sound card for a USB version of audio, but so far have had no issues. I was also apprehensive about giving up a set of heaphones with 8 individual speakers to one with just two, and Dolby headphone processing.    I bought the wired version of the Logitech 7.1 headphones, but returned them when I found the wireless pair weighed almost the same, and weren't much more expensive. I have been using them for a few weeks now, mostly playing Left4Dead2, watching movies, and listening to music.    First, the actual audible sound quality. Near perfect in my opinion. The audio is balanced and detailed, the bass doesn't overdrive the rest of the tones, and the mid-range isn't muddy. The multi-band EQ is a great touch that the wired version didn't have, and is great for music, but I didn't find myself enabling it in games.    Surround sound quality: Great. The \\"feel\\" of the surround sound is amazing, as far as the ability to detect where and how far a sound is coming from. In Left4Dead2 I can call out \\"smoker at three o-clock\\" or \\"charger behind us\\" without even looking. The only time the positioning seems to get a bit muddy is if something is RIGHT behind you, in which case it sort of sounds like \\"dead center\\" instead of \\"behind center\\". The surround sound even does pretty good with music, but I find myself turning it off for that. What is nice is changing between surround sound and stereo is a simple hard switch on the headphones, no need to open up aconfiguration application.    Microphone sound quality: Also great. The microphone is top-notch. You can turn on the voice repeat feature and hear it for your self. There are two issues with the microphone though. First, the mic does pick up a lot of outside sounds when your NOT talking. It picks up the smallest mouse click in a quiet room. It does however seem to compensate for loud rooms. At a LAN party it didn't pick up everything and only seemed to pick up voices, however not always my own voice. Second, even if the \\"side channel\\" sound some sound from the microphone is always fed back into the headphone signal. Even if you press the mute button on the headphones, if you scratch the surface of the mic you can head it in the audio. And it's and electrical connection too, not a vibration traveling up the headset. It does turn off entirely when the mic is \\"docked\\" at the top of the headset. Perhaps Logitech added this so you could hear yourself?     Noise isolation: The ear muffs on these are very good at cutting out outside noise. In fact I think Logitech put in the side channel control just so you could head someone nearby talk. This is great at a LAN party where you want to hear the game, but also bad when say someone is trying to get your attention.    Comfort: Despite being HUGE, and fairly heavy, the headphones are very comfortable, and I can wear them for hours besides one issue: They push on the arms of my glasses. Which many headphones do, but due to the noise isolation foam being pretty strong, it can start to wear on me.    Battery life: So far I have been able to game for many hours or more on the headphones with no issue, I never measured the exact amount. The manual says it will warn you before they stop working, however the one time I did run them to zero the warning was only seconds before they shut down. But since you can just plug in the USB and keep playing while charging, this really isn't an issue. They are wireless headphones 99% of the time.The do seem to take a few hours to charge. The software keeps a good readout of exactly how much battery you have left, and I often keep it open on a second monitor. The charging cord is long enough to work as a headphone cord.    Range: I was able to walk to the kitchen and get a drink without loosing audio., about 150ft and though four walls.    Cons and improvements:  -Side buttons could do a lot more. You can get some plugins but they have to be hard-coded and aren't very flexible.  -There is no real quick way to switch between USB audio for the headphones, and on-board audio for my speakers. I used a shortcut program and the side buttons to switch between them, but there could be a more elegant solution. For example, I would like it if I actually power off the headphones, the Logitech program could detect it and switch back to my secondary preferred sound output.  -More warning about impending battery failure would be great. When mine failed it was in the middle of a gauntlet event where I had zero time to find my charger cable. I had to finish the game without sound.  -An indicator when you have the voice changing enabled (perhaps blink the mic LED). Its a fun feature, but not when you get into a serious game and forgot you left it on, and five minutes in someone asks if your mic is broken.  -Remove the microphone audio entirely from the headphone sound.  -Provide a always-on setting. The number one complaint I see is from people who didn't have audio playing for 15 minutes, and then tried to play audio only to realize the headphones turned off automatically. Battery saving is great, but perhaps if the mic arm is down the headphones could stay on constantly?

0307477479|5.0|Egan follows the lives of several different characters over decades in this novel. The way she shifts the point of view from each character highlights the flow of time and the effect it has on all of us. Each of the characters we experience relate to one another in some way--as family, old friends, or a one time acquaintance.    The last chapter in particular was one of my favorites, as it raises some interesting questions in regards to the implications of technology, especially hand-held devices such as our cell phone computers. When discussing this section of the book at a recent event, held by Columbia College in Chicago, Egan said she was \\"picking up on things in the air and imagining forward.\\" Her imagination proved pretty accurate to the present day and our near future with children being inundated with technology from their birth. She foresaw,\\"class issues between off-the-grid people (without internet) being divided from those that have internet.\\" As a society we will notice people starting to fall through the cracks unable to get into the flow of information.    This point reminded me of a local news show (not Chicago) where the vapid reporter \\"took to the streets\\" to interview homeless people and had the idiocy to ask them if they'd heard of the media buzz surrounding a homeless man in another state that went viral on the internet for his radio voice. Then seemed shocked when they didn't know what she was talking about. Egan made one of her characters, Scotty, one of the disconnected. In complete irony he becomes a sort of icon because he is seen as mysterious since no one can look up any information on him.    Another feeling that is present in the novel is how we fall in love with certain times and memories in our lives, and how the nostalgia can either affect us as a fleeting memory or we can dwell on the past. Dwelling on the past can cause us to compare our trajectory in life to others that were once close, Scotty resents Benny when he hears that he has become a big-shot record producer while Scotty resorts to fishing in the city's river for food. It's a quick read and you'll love relating to how certain characters completely change over time. Overall this book is an entertaining, there is variety in the characters' personalities and points of view. Sometimes we even receive different characters' takes on a significant time period--which is always fun. I look forward to diving into some of Egan's other works.
B0013N4AT0|5.0|Still Life is an amazing piece of work.  It traverses a multitude of genres, but never completely leaves the death/black metal roots behind. I now find myself going back to Still Life more often than most of Opeth's other cd's, and rank it with Morningrise and Ghost Reveries in my top three. The recipe for a disaster film went like this; gather a bunch of big-name actors and put them in a highly dangerous situation together. Give each of the characters an interesting past, maybe some recurring problem that they solve in surviving the disaster. Throw in lots of high-budget scenes of destruction and death. These kinds of movies seriously cleaned up at the box offices, and made the big-name stars in the casts considerably wealthier.     TOWERING INFERNO is the best of the lot. B002HJ9PTO|3.0|I purchased 5 of these mics for a podcast I do. I use a mackie 16 channel mixer and wanted my other guests to have the freedom to move around on the couch and chairs without having to always keep distance from a standard mic. I found out about this mic from a gentleman who's using it in conjunction with a prosumer canon HD camcorder. The mic worked perfectly with this camera combination but when I got the mics from amazon and hooked them up to my professional mixer the volume was way below average. I had to turn my mic trim knobs up the entire way to get even a reasonable volume... and of course the noise floor came up as well - lot's of hiss and background.    I think this has to do with impedance and mic gain on mixers. Usually professional mixers prefer an xlr connection and a nominal mic volume. Camera's on the other hand use prosumer unbalanced connections - and account for this in chip design.    I'm keeping the mics because the lack of clutter and freedom to move outweigh the clarity of standard mics, however, I'm disappointed with the lack of headroom and having to turn the mic gain all the way up.    Also, I notice some steering in the audio. I listen to the main mix bus with headphones and i can hear the mic changing from mono to stereo. I know this is a mono mic but the mackie mixer doubles the signal for the main mix. I'm hoping that the battery might be going and a replacement will solve these issues.

My mother always told me not to marry a `handyman' because he would always say \\"I can fix that in 10 minutes\\" but never actually get around to it. This is her world.   The son Mitchell creates some controversy with his homosexual lifestyle, but personally I think it's good for America to see a family unit in this way. Him and his partner Cameron adopted a child from Vietnam and have `new parent' written all over their faces, but they are both very loving fathers.  I think the love and values expressed in this family are very admirable, and we should all strive to love a little more in our own lives. This show very cleverly shows us what a modern family can be with the right love and support, and I look forward to watching future episodes.

If you have a player more than a couple years old (unless you are using a PS3) you will probably be out of luck and will have to watch the DVD or buy a new BD player.  /  This is a very standard 3 disc release these days which means you get (mostly) the same thing in three different versions.  If you want all the special features the only way to get them is on the BD version.  Walmart has a DVD only version that has special features but it DOES NOT include the maximum movie material which makes up the bulk of the special features. This scenario is becoming more and more common (standard practice for Disney with every release) and if studios make money from it ... it isn't going away any time soon so do your homework before you buy a disc. I managed to eventually contact her to let her know how inspiring her work was, and I have since done one major collar with plans to do more (eventually, along with all my other projects!) I had the delightful experience of finally meeting the lady herself at the 2004 Bead and Button show. She really is one of nicest beaders creating and teaching and inspiring today. She delights in seeing what she had inspired in others, and is very happy that she has inspired so many people. And this book just continues that inspiration.    I think her instructions are clear enough that beginners can try some of the smaller projects, and of course intermediate and advanced beaders can also learn from this book. Just looking at how she puts divergent materials and colors together is worth the price of the book. I realize that Sherry probably has contacts in the gem and stone industry, and she may not be able to reveal them. Important characters are flat (but others are so wonderfully complex and rich - like Samuel Hamilton and Lee for instance!), and the story is very melodramatic and appeals too much to cheap sentimentality; that said, it is beautifully told and I highly recommend it.<br /><br />I wrote a more thorough review which you can read at my blog \\"52 Books in 52 Weeks - A personal quest to improve my writing in English.\\"

B000002UB6|5.0|As the Fab Four began to experience the disillusion that eventually overcame the hippie movement in the late '60s and early '70s, understanding that they were heading to different corners, they wanted to do something to inject new life into the band and maybe a vitality that would bring them together and carry them forward. The result was \\"Let It Be\\".<br />Although it ultimately didn't work to salvage the band, it is a remarkable work of art in it's own right and probably laid the foundation for their final, and what many consider to be their finest, album, \\"Abbey Road\\".<br />It still surprises many people when they hear that \\"Let It Be\\" was actually recorded before \\"Abbey Road\\". It is somewhat surprising to realize the contrasting sounds of the two albums are due, in large part, to the band's efforts to sound raw and live with \\"Let It Be\\" and their apparent acceptance that they wanted to end with a smoothly-engineered masterpiece such as \\"Abbey Road\\".<br />But getting back to \\"Let It Be\\", it is widely known and acknowledged that John, with the agreement of the band, wanted that raw, live sound to dominate the album. This led to the \\"rooftop\\" concert. It is a testament to the talent of the individuals involved that they could sound so cohesive and play so well in the cold and damp of those rooftop recordings. \\"Get Back\\", \\"Don't Let Me Down\\" (not included on this disk), \\"I've Got A Feeling\\", \\"One After Nine-O-Nine\\", \\"I Dig A Pony\\" all were played during this concert and much of the recorded material was mixed into the final versions released on the album.<br />In watching the film recorded of the band in the studio, those sessions were very loose and off-the-cuff, incorporating covers of old rock tunes; some Beatles tunes, some other covers. None of the arguing reported during the recording of the \\"White Album\\" was evident, but there were glimpses of friction or tension in some of the scenes on film. Overall, it appears that they are either having some fun or bored stiff, with little in between.<br />All of this relates to the sound of the album in that there are diverse directions in this collection of what amounted to over 30 hours of tape that sat for months, waiting to be edited. And while some of the material does sound raw and live, Phil Spector's handling of much of the material ended up making it sound polished and engineered. Not that either one, in itself, is a bad thing, but it speaks to the dysfunction that was going on in the band at that time. Paul has been quoted as saying that he didn't care for the orchestration of \\"Long And Winding Road\\", despite the fact that it may have been the biggest hit from the album. Some younger people might be able to relate the wishes and problems that the Beatles had with the sound of the album to the way Kurt Cobain felt about \\"Nevermind\\"; which he felt wasn't raw enough and was over-engineered, too polished.<br />Having said all that, it is actually my opinion that the diversity of the album is a perfect union of the talent involved and the divergent attitudes of the individuals. From the rawness of \\"I've Got A Feeling\\" to the polished beauty of the title cut \\"Let It Be\\", this album and the project surrounding it became the last public glimpse of what many people consider the greatest rock band ever.tle in between. B004EC5IUW|3.0|I'm a huge fan of the original series, which was an uncompromising personal artistic statement by its director, Hideaki Anno.  Despite its enduring popularity, it was not a series that pandered to a mass audience.  Those who say it fell apart at the end are sort of right, but the later release of the two movies \\"Death and Rebirth\\" and \\"The End of Evangelion\\" - which Anno has said restored his original vision for the series ending - redeemed that ending and properly completed an artistic masterpiece.    The two \\"Rebuild of Evangelion\\" films released so far are kind of an unnecessary update to the original series, condensed to the point of losing much of what made the original story so special and with alterations that in most cases seem gratuitous or even random.  This is even more true of Evangelion 2.22 than it was of 1.11.    For example, in the original series, the Eva pilots were all matched to a particular Eva unit, and there was a good reason for that (without getting into spoilers).  Eva pilots were physically and mentally incapable of piloting any Eva unit other than their own.  However, in Evangelion 2.22, pilots and Evas are mixed and matched seemingly at will, and new pilots and Evas are introduced that didn't exist in the original series, which are then mixed and matched too.  Unless both the reason and means for this are explained in later films, this seemingly destroys one of the central plot points of the entire series (the nature of the Evangelions).  There are many such examples of changes to the plot that some may not even notice at first, but which drastically alter what the series is all about.    Speaking of the characters, they've also been updated.  The main characters in the original series were all very different but all very rigid in their philosophies, and this lack of self awareness is part of what helped drive the plot forward and also one of the themes of the series.  In Evangelion 2.22, the characters universally mellow to the point that there's not much difference between them.  It's like the end of the Wizard of Oz; Shinji finds his strength, Asuka finds her heart, Rei finds her humanity.  That's nice, and probably something a certain segment of fans wanted to see, but it's going to make the later films (there are 2 more scheduled) a lot less interesting.  Also, there's a new character and Eva pilot named Mari, who at least in this film is completely disposable.  Meanwhile, Shinji's classmates, who played a major role in the series, play no role at all in the plot of this film and simply take up screen time that could have been used to better develop the plot, or Mari's character.    The film is beautiful to look at - like Evangelion 1.11, it maintains the visual style of the original series while updating almost every shot to CGI.  It may not be obvious in every shot, but many shots were created in CG and then processed to look like film, with a judder effect.  On the other hand, there are a *lot* of \\"fan service\\" shots in this film, which may be good or bad depending on your point of view.    I won't give away any spoilers but the ending of the film also leaves me wondering where they go from here, and how they're going to manage to fill out two more complete films.  They're basically already halfway through \\"The End of Evangelion\\".  I think they simply tried to do too much in 2 hours of screen time.    Overall, as a big fan of the series, it's nice to see these characters again and it's nice to have something new going on, but if you felt the original series was flawed, the current film series is even more flawed.  And while the original series was more or less one man's artistic vision, the current films feel more like filmmaking by committee with maximum box office receipts in mind.

I enjoyed Orson Scott Card's vision of the future, where survival of the human species leans not only on combatting the bugger threat, but developing a system of population control, orbital colonies, and planetary habitats. More so, however, I truly enjoyed Card's opinions on utility and utilitarianism demonstrated in the narrative.    Ender Wiggin, as we know him, is a five or six year old boy at the beginning of the novel. Despite his young age, he demonstrates more maturity than a well-travelled, experienced, world-weary man. He knows he is a tool, and will be treated like one by the government agency tasked with sharpening his edge. He is also extremely aware that this sharpening process requires time, and more so - pain, of both the most excruciating physical and mental nature alike.    There is a powerful message here, something that goes beyond the realm of the characters, the plot, and the setting.     While I won't tell you how to feel about it, I encourage you to read Ender's Game, and allow it to probe what you consider right and wrong.    You never know when the buggers may arrive.

The sample tracks that I heard from RDLS just didn't grab me, and I feared that the pop monster had finally swallowed Jamiroquai whole.   While this doesn't stack up with the first 3 albums, for me it's at least AS good as Dynamite and Synkronized, and definitely better than A Funk Odyssey.  I'm not going to say that this album is a return to form, but I will say a couple of things it has going for it:  1) Greater bench strength - there's not a track on the album that I hate, which hasn't happened since Traveling without Moving I think.  2) Stretching out a bit - some of the songs use odd time signatures, switch gears in the middle, and generally take some risks - there's very little here that is radio-friendly in my opinion.  The experiments don't always work, but at least he's trying.  What it doesn't have:  1) Even a handful of songs that make you want to replay them right away.   The only one that has stuck with me is \\"Two Completely Different Things\\", which is really one of those warm soul major-key shimmering-guitar tracks that I am always a sucker for (see: Seven Days in Sunny June).   Other than that, there's plenty of agreeable stuff, but nothing that makes you go \\"damn that was great\\".   So while the diversions from the pop template are welcome, they aren't necessarily inspired either.    So,for the reviewers that say this is the best Jami album in a long time, that's probably faint praise, but I tend to agree.  For the people that say that this is a lazy attempt that isn't worthy of the name Jamiroquai, I disagree with them but I see the traits that make them say that.       Overall, I think if you are a long-term fan of Jamiroquai that loves the Stevie Wonderish 70's soul sound and have been on the fence about picking this up (in the U.S., this is not a straightforward decision due to the import price), I would say go for it, you'll be happy you did in the end.  If you're new to Jamiroquai, probably best to start someplace else like Return of the Space Cowboy or Traveling Without Moving.  If you're the type of Jamiroquai fan that really likes the dance-pop stuff....you'll likely think this album is kind of boring.

1604443502|4.0|Down and Out in Paris and London, Orwell's first published book, is a masterpiece that depicts with clear, honest language, the true condition of the urban poor in the 1930s. Structured more like a memoir than a novel, the first half describes the conditions of the poor in Paris, the unnamed narrator meets a series of down and out (though rarely less than eccentric) types in dingy Paris hotels, and eventually finds employment as a Plongeur - dishwasher, in a series of hotel restaurants. This gives him the opportunity to experience life as an unpaid drudge. Working the same dirty repetitive job, poorly paid, no union to protect him (the reason for this lack of representation is clearly explained in the book), gives the narrator scope to explain the mind sapping routine of the manual worker. He concludes with a rallying call to arms about the pointlessness of the Plongeur's work - how he cannot even claim it is socially useful. All he is doing is serving (usually badly and inefficiently), the desires of the wealthier clientele that frequent the hotels, often themselves being ripped off in the process.<br /><br />The second part of the book is devoted to the author's native England. There, he frequents similarly low life condition in the spikes and hostels frequented by tramps and beggars in Kent and London. He meets Bozo - a tramp and philosopher and several other low lifes, their stories each unique and fascinating such as the fellow old Etonian, drunk on cherry brandy, expensive brogues poking out of the filthy hostel bed sheets, but all united by a desperate, ruinous poverty.<br /><br />Orwell is unsparing in his description of filth, discomfort and squalor of the tramps' lifestyle, and is magnificent on the details - the practice of picking up used cigarette ends to try and form a miserable cigarette of their own, the hand to mouth existence of the pavement scrievers on Embankment, and the evolution of street slang.<br /><br />His evocations of life literally on the breadline (bread and marge, or cheese was the standard tramps' fare, many were little short of starving), are harrowing. In Paris he explains that there is a certain liberation in only having two francs in your pocket as you can't plan more than a day ahead. How far Orwell was genuinely reduced to this condition we should question. Biographers refer to the stipends he received from family members, which allowed him to pursue, relatively autonomously, his writing career. His destitute nature may have been largely simulated to gather material for his book, rather than a genuine reduction to destitution. This doesn't devalue the book, but perhaps accounts for it not warranting the full 5 stars, given that an account from a person who genuinely faced lifelong poverty would have that extra harrowing bite to it.nd marge, or cheese was the standard tramps' fare, many were little short of starving), are harrowing.
