1400052181|5.0|The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks by Rebecca Skloot is a story long overdue.  In parts, it was fascinating, heartbreaking, outrageous and frightening--but ultimately uplifting.  Skloot's book is actually several different stories, and she weaves them together seamlessly.  There is the story of Henrietta Lacks, her family, her cells and the ethics involved in medical research.<br /><br />Henrietta Lacks was a 30 year-old black woman when she was diagnosed with cervical cancer in 1951.  Living in Baltimore with her husband and 5 children, she sought treatment at Johns Hopkins University Hospital.  She died 9 months later.  Before her death, her doctor took samples of her cells--both normal and cancerous.  The cells were given to Hopkins researcher, Dr. George O. Gey.  Gey was not only able to keep the cancerous cells alive, but they actually started reproducing--the first time this was seen in human cells.  Gey was generous with these cells and before long, researchers from all over the world were using Henrietta's cells (now called HeLa) for research.  Many scientists claim that HeLa cells were the most important medical breakthrough in the 20th Century.<br /><br />Over the years, HeLa cells \\"helped develop drugs for treating herpes, leukemia, influenza, hemophilia, and Parkinson's disease; and they've been used to study lactose digestion, sexually transmitted diseases, appendicitis, human longevity, mosquito mating, and the negative cellular effects of working in sewers.\\"  They've been exposed to radiation, and used to test \\"steroids, chemotherapy drugs, hormones, vitamins, and environmental stress...\\"  They've been sent into space and used to test cosmetics.  They've been used to study genetics, they've been cloned, and they were used to create hybrids.  The U.S. Patent and Trademark Office lists over 17,000 patents using HeLa.  Today, HeLa cells have evolved into a multibillion dollar business.<br /><br />It wasn't until over 20 years after Henrietta's death that her family became aware that her cells were still alive, being used for research and making companies lots of money.  When the author was 16, her science teacher introduced Skloot to Henrietta Lacks and her cells, although he didn't know much about Henrietta.  So Skloot decided that Henrietta's story needed to be told.  But Henrietta's family had major reservations about Skloot and her motives and at first, refused to work with her.  \\"It's not only the story of HeLa cells and Henrietta Lacks, but of Henrietta's family--particularly Deborah [daughter]--and their lifelong struggle to make peace with the existence of those cells, and the science that made them possible.\\"  It also seems terribly wrong to them that while many have become rich off their mother, they are poor, uninsured, and can't afford the drugs that were most likely created using HeLa cells.<br /><br />The Immortal Life also deals with medical ethics, consent, medical experiments on humans, cell ownership, and who has a right to profit from your cells.  The tissues taken from Henrietta were taken without her consent.  Some of her cancer cells were injected into unknowing patients in medical experiments.  There have been gains made over the years to protect patients, but there is always a battle between patients and researchers when changes are proposed.<br /><br />The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks is a book that will haunt you long after you're done.  I can't recommended it enough.e aware that her cells were still alive, being used for research and making companies lots of money.  When the author was 16, her science teacher introduced Skloot to Henrietta Lacks and her cells, although he didn't know much about Henrietta.  So Skloot decided that Henrietta's story needed to be told.  But Henrietta's family had major reservations about Skloot and her motives and at first, refused to work with her.  \\"It's not only the story of HeLa cells and Henrietta Lacks, but of Henrietta's family--particularly Deborah [daughter]--and their lifelong struggle to make peace with the existence of those cells, and the science that made them possible.\\"  It also seems terribly wrong to them that while many have become rich off their mother, they are poor, uninsured, and can't afford the drugs that were most likely created using HeLa cells.      The Immortal Life also deals with medical ethics, consent, medical experiments on humans, cell ownership, and who has a right to profit from your cells.  The tissues taken from Henrietta were taken without her consent.  Some of her cancer cells were injected into unknowing patients in medical experiments.  There have been gains made over the years to protect patients, but there is always a battle between patients and researchers when changes are proposed.    The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks is a book that will haunt you long after you're done.  I can't recommended it enough.

B003WUYOQ6|4.0|I'll get the one major negative about this book out of the way first: it sorta beats the reader over the head with anecdotal evidence about its topic. Someone needs to tell the author that the plural of \\"anecdote\\" is not \\"data\\". Fortunately, it ALSO extensively cites published scientific studies and reputable professionals.     The book consists of two parts, which correspond to its title. The first part is about why we get fat. It provides extensive discussion of two primary points: that excessive consumption of carbohydrates is the cause of fatness, and that losing weight by counting calories and exercising doesn't really work very well at taking off or (especially) keeping off the fat. (As any fat person can attest.)     The second part was most interesting to me: what to do about it. It begins with a relatively extensive but quite readable discussion of how the body burns and stores the nutrition it gets from food. This is very important to understanding the rest of the book. It's also very informative - my doctor has been talking to me about things like HDL and LDL choleterol, or triglicerides, and almost all I knew about them was that he told me they were good or bad and gave me some numbers. After reading this book I understand what they are, what they do, and WHY they're good or bad, which helps me to take control of my health.    It then discusses, in terms of what the reader just learned about their body, the biochemical mechanism of what causes people to gain fat and lose fat, demonstrating WHY carbohydrates make us fat, and what other negative health consequences come from eating too much carbohydrates - including diabetes, high blood pressure, high bad cholesterol, low good cholesterol, gout, and increased chances of heart attacks and cancer.    The book then proposes a solution - a diet based primarily around eating meat. (It explains that vegetables can be fine too, and that different people can eat different amounts of carbohydrates while losing, orat least not gaining, fat, but these are incidental and not the primary topic of the book.) It then examines what the implications of this proposed diet are biochemically to demonstrate how it works, and then it discusses the health implications of this proposed diet to show that a diet consisting primarily of fatty meat (the book is very clear that eating nothing but lean meat is unhealthy, and that fat is required) can be surprisingly healthy.     It also examines the question of how, if we know that the standard \\"count calories and exercise\\" diet doesn't really work, some people lose weight that way. It argues that they lose weight because in reducing their overall calories they are also reducing their carbohydrate intake, and losing weight because of that, not because of the starvation and exercise they are torturing themselves with. The implication is that a reduced carbohydrate diet is actually the ONLY way to lose fat, and that calorie-counting diets only actually work if (and because) they approximate a reduced carbohydrate diet.    I found this book to be simultaneously helpful and disturbing. It's helpful because it significantly contributes to my understanding of my own health and my ability to make informed decisions about my diet. It's disturbing because it plainly shows that most of what we're taught, and indeed most of what I've been taught all of my life, about nutrition is plainly wrong, and the truth is almost completely opposite. There's a brief part in the book that both made me laugh and really shook me in which the author carefully lays out (apparently as an extreme example to demonstrate his case) why we would be healthier if we replaced all the carbohydrates in our diet with an equal amount of lard.    I'm recommending this book to all of my loved ones and urging them to please read it right away.

Haller is a slick, shady L.A. criminal defense lawyer who gets results for his low-life clients, even if things do not always turn out for the best.  Haller runs into serious problems, suffers blows from them, does not act always and mindlessly cocky (unlike the annoying, one-note young prosecutor-on-the-make in the movie Fracture), yet draws on enough strength, ingenuity, and resourcefulness that his confidence rarely fails him.  This made him fun to watch.  Sometimes, he stepped over the line, but not so far as to throw believability to the winds.  The story was basically interesting and tied up fairly neatly. Lincoln Lawyer was refreshingly free of exploitative, sensational scenes used to gin up cheap shock value, horror, suspense, or titillation.    At the same time, I have to agree with the critics that the movie's story and characters do seem familiar.  Although workmanlike, including the courtroom scenes, well-handled by McConaughey, the movie lacked the sparks of imagination, originality, intricacy, and electricity that can sometimes come from films like this. Haller's interactions with his ex-wife were not convincing.  Similarly unconvincing was the degree to which he felt a crisis of conscience about the way he represented a past client in light of newly discovered information from representing his current, rich-boy client, accused of trying to beat to death a woman he met at a bar.  Haller had more practical and selfish reasons for resolving the current case the way he did, without delving so deeply into soul-searching and noble motives, which seemed ill-suited to the rest of the movie.

This speaks volumes to the heiress shopping with daddy's credit card that has no limit or expiration date (Paris Hilton), as well as, to the struggling college student, attending classes full time and working a 40+ hour week (Me). It changes the viewpoint of working hard and fighting to survive, beating yourself to death with extra shifts and a never ending supply of Ramen Noodles, all while chasing a dream promised but never delivered. As pessimistic as this may seem, Gladwell delivers a claim sure to wake up the rich and encourage the poor. There is a fortitude instilled into our minds after Gladwell shares his personal story, a tragic account of poverty and degradation. And through all of his pessimism and depressing findings of success being predetermined, he challenges the unpriviledged to not be the statistic, but instead, to fight to be the exception.

0307593495|5.0|Kurt Wallander is getting old and being honest about it. His own mortality is reflected in the death around him, and in the birth of his granddaughter.  Politics is more foregrounded in this novel than the others; people who have lived through the wars of the 1960s up through the collapse of the Soviet Union will appreciate the inferences of just how nasty international espionage was, how over-the-top, how stacked with illusions.  The unlayering of a political murder in this novel is also an unlayering of the melancholy Wallander.  It's a moving book, and one more example of how the borders of crime fiction and \\"mainstream\\" fiction have become blurred in a world that is increasingly criminal.    According to the author this is the last Wallander book.  I hope that Wallander's daughter, Linda, will take the baton; and I hope the aging retired Wallander will be kept as a character and fatherly adviser.     A great read.I think it's his best.
B0029LHWFO|4.0|It took me a while to read this book, despite the good reviews. In fact, I just finished it (2011). Jack Cashill's accusations of plagiarism prompted me to look at it for myself. Apparently, it's not enough for Obama to advocate fascism/socialism and kicking bunnies in the stomach. He's also got to be a terrible person. How effin' juvenile. Cashill charges that Bill Ayers actually wrote the book. I've got Ayers's Fugitive Days waiting at the library and I hope the Cashill comes in by the time I finish it so that I can go over the specific argument.    Reading over the comments, it strikes me that people's political views, left and right, affect their perception of the book. Those who get \\"Obama hates white people\\" differ from my own reading. I admit I'm on the left. However, I have never thought the President walks on water. Nevertheless, I want to say something about the book as a book, rather than as a political litmus test.    First of all, it's a rather curious memoir, in that you don't find out a lot about Barack Obama's inner life. One senses a characteristic reserve, displayed also in his interviews, an unwillingness to give away too much. Indeed, he seems far more interested in other people than in himself. Indeed, he's much more penetrating of others. He tends to see things through a sociological, rather than a personal lens, despite the recounting of personal experience. The personal gets filtered through the sociological. People are often seen as instances of general problems.    The prose is better than usual from a politician, and thoroughly consistent with the language of Obama's early speeches. In fact, more than a few passages in the book sound like parts of speeches. However, this is a politician/lawyer's default genre. My favorite section was his account of his trip to Kenya to see his father's extended family. There's no romanticizing of the place. Indeed, Obama recognizes the dangers of such an attitude. But he doesn't swing to the other extreme,either. He tries to take a realistic look at his family and at Kenya.    Most important, however, is the narrative's overall movement. Obama begins as a stranger to himself, which is probably another reason why he doesn't reveal a lot. At the time, he probably didn't know a lot. The book is a search and negotiation for identity. To Obama's credit, he has plenty of opportunity to settle into some preset world-view. In fact, the temptations are pretty great. But he's honest enough with himself to realize that they ultimately won't satisfy him. He has to work out his own salvation. By the end of the book, he may not have arrived at a final triumph, but he definitely has turned a corner.

1555175163|4.0|A readable, concise bio of Ip Man: reverential, descriptive, with anecdotes that reveal his personality, approach, and philosophy. Ip Man is a unique individual but also a full-blown archetype of the old-school Chinese martial arts grandmaster: proudly Chinese, rather traditional, a lettered renaissance man (equally skilled in Chinese calligraphy), well-mannered, genuinely kind, charming, and extraordinarily skilled and effective despite a small physique (which strengthens wing chun's message about its effectiveness against larger opponents). As a morality tale, I especially enjoyed the late anecdote about how Ip Man strove, in his later years, to impart his full skills to selected students and how one student showed his care for his Sifu who was under considerable strain.    Authored by son Ip Ching, there is much personal credibility, though more critical readers may notice (and, I suggest, forgive) the usual hagiographical biases. On the latter, hagiographical inclinations are a common feature of the martial arts biography genre, and Ip Man's historical stature in the wing chun world means that in the vast majority of bios out there, he can do no wrong. That said, to my knowledge and research, Ip Man is the real deal, much loved by students for his class and kindness as much as he was admired for his skill.    [Disclosure: I am a dedicated Jeet Kune Do martial artist, not wing chun trained, but I have a deep respect for wing chun and consider it the mother art and the original soul of Jeet Kune Do. I approach the Ip Man literature with much respect and also objectivity.]

0316017922|4.0|Read _Outliers_ and become the next Bill Gates! (maybe)     This book is a much longer version of the old adage: \\"Luck (or success) is where preparation meets opportunity.\\"     Yes, but how much preparation? And how do you find the right opportunity? Gladwell's answer: about 10,000 hours of practice should prepare you for that moment when opportunity comes-a-knocking (i.e. Bill Gates put in 10,000 hours of computer programming, the Beatles put in 10,000 hours of stage time before getting a record deal).     This opportunity arises from a hodgepodge of culture, birth date, time period, and where someone is \\"from.\\" For example, of the one hundred richest men on the planet (ever), many like Rockefeller and Carnegie, were born in mid-19th Century America (1830-ish). Random? Gladwell thinks not.    These men may seem like \\"outliers\\" (freaks of nature who just got lucky), but really they were both in the right place at the right time AND they also put in a heck of a lot of hard work. When the industrial revolution began, they were primed and ready to become millionaires. Similarly, if you happened to be born around 1955 and you had a family who encouraged you to study (a lot), and you had unlimited access to computers (as a teenager) and you enjoyed computer programming (a relatively new phenomenon), you could also be like Bill Gates.     The only downside is that if you want to jump on the \\"outlier\\" train, it seems you must have some sense of clairvoyance to know when (as Tasler said in his review) the next industrial revolution or silicon age might arise, otherwise you might waste 10,000 hours on the wrong bright idea (8 Tracks anyone?).    The best part about this book is not even the seemingly researched, poignantly delivered anecdotal stories, but the fact that Gladwell makes it feel like he's writing just to you. Hockey fan? He's got a story for ya. Aviation junkie? Rock and roll aficionado? He's got you covered too.     Essentially, Gladwell wants to make you re-think the definition of success. Some questions came to mind:         -What if we acknowledged each other's cultural differences rather than shove them under politically correct rugs?       -What if we built relationships and encouraged each other to put in 10,000 hours of work towards our passions?        - What people had equal access to preparation and opportunity?     Read the book, join this discussion, and let's never stop asking the hard questions...

0156030306|4.0|Although there is no getting around the fact that the short story upon which this novel is based is much better, this is still a fabulous tale about the mind, fabulously told.  Latter-day readers will draw parallels with Forest Gump, but of course this tale came first.  The story is told from the point of view of an adult with the IQ of an eight- or nine-year-old boy. Selected for an experiment that *may* increase his intelligence,  the boy begins journalling his thoughts.  The reader experiences his meteoric rise to genius status as told in his own words, which become increasingly erudite with each passing week.  The question of whether emotional maturity and intellectual maturity are at all correlated may seem a bit of a foregone conclusion, but the reactions of those involved in the experiment to their new creation are nuanced, as are those of the experimentee.  Any science fiction fan should have this on his/her bookshelf, but it is an  important enough work that others who like to think outside the box of everyday human existence should pick it up, too.

Whether his depiction of Bill Gates, The Beatles, or even hockey players is relevant to you or not, he connects with his audience. Am I an undiscovered outlier that has beaten many odds? Actually, Gladwell recognizes the many challenges or advantages we are all offered while struggling to realize the dreams we work so hard for. You might even calculate the hours you have put into your own dream like I did.  Outliers greatly surprised me with its message and I encourage you to read it and recognize your own success story!

B004IFYN2Y|4.0|An excellent combination of true life thriller and drama \\"Fair Game\\" portrays what happened when CIA Agent Valerie Plame Wilson was \\"outed\\" after her husband Ambassador Joe Wilson wrote the New York Times editorial entitled \\"What I Didn't Find In Africa\\" questioning the discovery of material to make nuclear devices which provided part of the rational for President George W. Bush going to war in Iraq. What's fascinating about this dramatic thriller is that it recalls films like \\"All the President's Men\\" in terms of its matter-of-fact presentation and the reaction of our government to try and minimize or discredit people doing what they felt was right.<br /><br />The resulting fall out (pun intended) resulted in his wife being exposed as an agent, putting those under her supervision and those she was trying to help as part of her ops at risk all for political gain. The result was that even Wilson himself faced accusations that he was lying for financial gain in the process. The resulting pressures nearly drove them apart destroying their lives all for the sake of saving face.<br /><br />The film directed by Doug Liman (\\"The Bourne Identity\\") and written by Jez and John Henry-Butterworth but based on the book by Plame benefits from strong and authentic feeling performances by Naomi Watts as Plame and Sean Penn as Wilson.<br /><br />The Blu-ray edition of the film features a strong looking, sharp transfer. Colors don't exactly pop but they aren't meant to here--the film is true to the original theatrical presentation in that regard but detail remains razor sharp throughout the presentation of the film. Audio is quite strong although keep in mind this is primarily a dialogue driven drama.<br /><br />Unfortunately the only special feature included on the Blu-ray is a commentary track by Plame and Wilson and while it is a welcome addition, it would have been interesting to have licensed some of the news footage of Plame's actual testimony about the events that transpired. The duo admit that it's a bit weird commenting on a drama based on real life that they lived through (since there will be a certain amount of fictionalized elements to make it work as a film). It might have been better to have both of them discuss the making of the film with director Liman and comment on the process of life vs. film.<br /><br />An engrossing drama, \\"Fair Game\\" reminds us all that there are real casualities when personal reputations are attacked as part of a political cover up. I imagine that your reaction to the film may be influenced also by your politics as well although given what occurred it shouldn't. What was done to Plame, Wilson and those that depended on them was wrong.<br /><br />Recommended.weird commenting on a drama based on real life that they lived through (since there will be a certain amount of fictionalized elements to make it work as a film).
